        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer186850889" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18151455084757139590">You will love it or hate it, I am in the former category. What to expect? lack of chapters, abrupt changes in PoV, crazy characters and crazier ones, a consistent thought that a guiding hand has a role in everything, R rated humor, and lots of drunk fairies. If you don't think you can handle that, don't bother.<br/><br/>But I love this book. Punk rock is better with fiddles, the streets of New York probably resemble a Greek battleground more than most of us know, and entire societies shouldn't be complete</span>
  <span id="freeText18151455084757139590" style="display:none">You will love it or hate it, I am in the former category. What to expect? lack of chapters, abrupt changes in PoV, crazy characters and crazier ones, a consistent thought that a guiding hand has a role in everything, R rated humor, and lots of drunk fairies. If you don't think you can handle that, don't bother.<br /><br />But I love this book. Punk rock is better with fiddles, the streets of New York probably resemble a Greek battleground more than most of us know, and entire societies shouldn't be completely industrialized in one generation. <br /><br />Ill never be able to explain what happens, even after my third reread. But hey, it has a introduction by Neil Gaiman, so that should be good enough for you. Just imagine American Gods with humor, crazy chapter structure, more drunken fairies, and half the length.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18151455084757139590" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3148700576237046289" style="display:none">I was recently at a Nerdy Girl game night where we played a few rounds of a kickstarter game called <a href="http://bringyourownbook.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Bring Your Own Book</a> which is a book lover's version of Apples to Apples or Cards Against Humanity. This book got thrown in the mix of books we were using to play the game, and the phrases we pulled out of it were always eye-popping. They were such an irreverent, strange, kinky, what-the-hell mix of phrases that we were all wanting to read the book by night's end. We decided we're going to read it together in May, so I promptly snatched up a copy. The book has a forward by Neil Gaiman in which he says, "Read it now, and then make your friends buy their own copies. You'll thank me someday." I'm not sure if I'll hate or love the book, but it sounds like fun. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10038267147619275692">The writing style was light and easy to read. This made it a quick read but also left the story very shallow. There were a larger than average number of characters and the story rapidly switched between them. Each time I picked the book up again I had trouble remembering what was happening with all of the characters.<br/><br/>The premise of the book seemed very funny, fairies running amok in New York City, but the delivery was only mildly humorous and overly crass in places. The characters themselves lack</span>
  <span id="freeText10038267147619275692" style="display:none">The writing style was light and easy to read. This made it a quick read but also left the story very shallow. There were a larger than average number of characters and the story rapidly switched between them. Each time I picked the book up again I had trouble remembering what was happening with all of the characters.<br /><br />The premise of the book seemed very funny, fairies running amok in New York City, but the delivery was only mildly humorous and overly crass in places. The characters themselves lacked depth and their conflicts were not very interesting, in the end I didn't really care what happened to the characters.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1774824810" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1774824810" />
    <a title="Dafydd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4331319-dafydd"><img alt="Dafydd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469139061p2/4331319.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1774824810?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dafydd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dafydd" href="/user/show/4331319-dafydd">Dafydd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4331319-dafydd?shelf=sci-fi-fantasy">sci-fi-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1774824810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2895200380612576235">3.5 stars, rounded up to 4.<br/><br/>The writing is a mess. One reviewer said something to the effect of this book feels like it was written by someone who's 14, and I don't entirely disagree. It's writing for someone with serious ADHD--it jumps back and forth between a large cast of characters, sometimes within the same paragraph, and the ending is downright ridiculous. This makes it an often frustrating read, and there seems to be little depth or emotional life to the characters.<br/><br/>Yet! Somehow the author </span>
  <span id="freeText2895200380612576235" style="display:none">3.5 stars, rounded up to 4.<br /><br />The writing is a mess. One reviewer said something to the effect of this book feels like it was written by someone who's 14, and I don't entirely disagree. It's writing for someone with serious ADHD--it jumps back and forth between a large cast of characters, sometimes within the same paragraph, and the ending is downright ridiculous. This makes it an often frustrating read, and there seems to be little depth or emotional life to the characters.<br /><br />Yet! Somehow the author has imbued them with a humanness I find lacking in most recent fiction. They are petty, superficial, short-sighted, immature, selfish, and frequently do very stupid things. Which is to say, they feel human (even the fairies). And, for all the frustration of the choppy structure, I think it adds to this; it reflects the turbulence of life for the characters, and the light tone of this format matches the light tone of the flighty (pun intended) tone of the characters as well.<br /><br />And this is certainly a light book. This is not Dostoevsky; anyone expecting a serious approach to life or character will surely be disappointed. And yet! again. The frustrating, choppy, maddeningly superficial approach conceals a depth that is easy to miss among all the narrative jumps and shallow characters. Most notably, this is easily one of the most sympathetic, authentic depictions of what it's like to be poor, and how it affects people and their choices, that I've read that's been published after the early 1900s. Do the characters behave in ridiculous, often self-sabotaging ways? Certainly. But this is treated sympathetically rather than judgmentally. I can barely remember reading any fiction written post-Dickens that even acknowledges poverty, let alone depicts characters dealing with it in ways that feel realistic and relatable. Certainly not anything that was actually enjoyable to read--what few novels I recall that treat poverty seriously are dour. Even most fantasy novels, where the hardscrabble character living on the streets or working in the scullery is a trope, rarely treats them in a way that shows any kind of awareness of the effects of poverty on one's psychology and choices. But Martin Millar gets it right.<br /><br />I guess in summary, I'd say it's a book that can be highly frustrating to read, and I do think there are many readers to whom it won't appeal, but I still found it overall both fun and poignant. If you open it up and, after maybe ten pages find the characters or narrative structure irritating, put it down. Otherwise, keep reading.<br /><br />*Also, the publisher did a lousy job at proofing this one--there are typos liberally sprinkled throughout. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2895200380612576235" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2418461362" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2418461362" />
    <a title="Diana Skelton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5855343-diana-skelton"><img alt="Diana Skelton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597164431p2/5855343.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2418461362?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diana Skelton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diana Skelton" href="/user/show/5855343-diana-skelton">Diana Skelton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2418461362" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13929765038373859717">Sorry to those who liked this book, but many things about it grated for me. There's a lot of repetition, both of plot points (the unending disappearances of significant objects just when they have almost been recovered) and of turns of phrase (about the smell of vomit from drunken fairies). A point is made about there being no such thing as homophobia among fairies--but the book's fairies are at least as prone as humans are to violent racist brawls, economic exploitation, and working as mercenar</span>
  <span id="freeText13929765038373859717" style="display:none">Sorry to those who liked this book, but many things about it grated for me. There's a lot of repetition, both of plot points (the unending disappearances of significant objects just when they have almost been recovered) and of turns of phrase (about the smell of vomit from drunken fairies). A point is made about there being no such thing as homophobia among fairies--but the book's fairies are at least as prone as humans are to violent racist brawls, economic exploitation, and working as mercenaries. Why homophobia is the only fault they avoid is left unexplained. There's also a contrast drawn between one human's obsession with porn and the fairies' casual acceptance of incest -- again, with no discernible point made.<br />Although all of this made me want to stop reading, I persisted in hopes of learning the reason that so many homeless people die throughout the novel. However, this too is left unexplained in the end. Several characters voice regret for the lives of the homeless people (and two characters are homeless humans). But the fact that the mystery behind this thread seemed to drop away completely at the end left me even more disappointed than before with the book as a whole.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13929765038373859717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Audrey Greathouse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15622802-audrey-greathouse"><img alt="Audrey Greathouse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450999410p2/15622802.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1581593904?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Audrey Greathouse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Audrey Greathouse" href="/user/show/15622802-audrey-greathouse">Audrey Greathouse</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1581593904" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17757364246452382367">An absolutely brilliant piece of humor writing. I've never seen Neil Gaiman recommend a book so passionately, nor run into a book that was so under-appreciated. If you like Kurt Vonnegut, Douglas Adams, Terry Pratchet, or Neil himself, you simply must give this book a try. The great news is that it opens with the best first sentence in all of literature and only gets better from there...but if you don't like it by the first three-page chapter, I'll eat my hat. Wander around New York with fugitiv</span>
  <span id="freeText17757364246452382367" style="display:none">An absolutely brilliant piece of humor writing. I've never seen Neil Gaiman recommend a book so passionately, nor run into a book that was so under-appreciated. If you like Kurt Vonnegut, Douglas Adams, Terry Pratchet, or Neil himself, you simply must give this book a try. The great news is that it opens with the best first sentence in all of literature and only gets better from there...but if you don't like it by the first three-page chapter, I'll eat my hat. Wander around New York with fugitive Scottish fairies, a beautiful woman with a horrible disease, a horrible man who will put you at dis-ease, and a homeless woman who also happens to be an ancient Athenian general. Everyone's apathy and emotions go at a million miles an hour, and it has a Shakespearean air to the way it's always one thing after another with these fools.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17757364246452382367" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('aHjzPeXVKHY2+MRxZ89fnA5c4IUGTiMlaJebkk80jIj8sO+3ubtYBXSN8zO1nD7jNRZ0+Ad6GT/nmKuFTt9lYA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NUdgKzTKlAYaOCsc9u5GHknbiCvOeAYJGobcKg5tHTmhj3yhaKTkdVhNHF4kvSdhcpEcVs9MPBOView9D4b00Q==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Egeus, the Duke’s minister, is furious that his daughter Hermia is determined to marry Demetrius, instead of Lysander, the man he has chosen for her. The Duke decrees that if, by the time of the wedding, Hermia still refuses to marry Lysander, she shall be executed . . . or consigned to a nunnery. Pocket, being Pocket, cannot help but point out that this decree is complete bollocks, and that the Duke is an egregious weasel for having even suggested it. Irritated by the fool’s impudence, the Duke orders his death. With the Duke’s guards in pursuit, Pocket makes a daring escape.\n\nHe soon stumbles into the wooded realm of the fairy king Oberon, who, as luck would have it, IS short a fool. His jester Robin Goodfellow—the mischievous sprite better known as Puck—was found dead. Murdered. Oberon makes Pocket an offer he can’t refuse: he will make Pocket his fool and have his death sentence lifted if Pocket finds out who killed Robin Goodfellow. But as anyone who is even vaguely aware of the Bard’s most performed play ever will know, nearly every character has a motive for wanting the mischievous sprite dead.\n\nWith too many suspects and too little time, Pocket must work his own kind of magic to find the truth, save his neck, and ensure that all ends well.\n\nA rollicking tale of love, magic, madness, and murder, Shakespeare for Squirrels is a Midsummer Night’s noir—a wicked and brilliantly funny good time conjured by the singular imagination of Christopher Moore.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14221133702766804659\" href=\"#\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16103361449852359860">Martin Millar is a critically acclaimed Scottish writer from Glasgow, now resident in London. He also writes the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/55517-thraxas' rel='nofollow noopener'>Thraxas</a> series of fantasy novels under the pseudonym Martin Scott.<br/><br/>The novels he writes as Martin Millar dwell on urban decay and British sub-cultures, and the impact this has on a range of characters, both realistic and supernatural. There are elements of magical realism, and the feelin</span>
  <span id="freeText16103361449852359860" style="display:none">Martin Millar is a critically acclaimed Scottish writer from Glasgow, now resident in London. He also writes the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/55517-thraxas" rel="nofollow noopener">Thraxas</a> series of fantasy novels under the pseudonym Martin Scott.<br /><br />The novels he writes as Martin Millar dwell on urban decay and British sub-cultures, and the impact this has on a range of characters, both realistic and supernatural. There are elements of magical realism, and the feeling that the boundary between real life and the supernatural is not very thick. Most of them are set in Brixton, Millar's one-time place of residence. Many are at least semi-autobiographical, and Love and Peace with Melody Paradise and Suzy, Led Zeppelin and Me both feature Millar himself as a character.<br /><br />As Martin Scott his Thraxas novels are a fusion of traditional high fantasy and pulp noir thrillers.<br /><br />In 2000, he won the World Fantasy Award for best novel for <i>Thraxas</i>.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16103361449852359860" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
